  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_f19933f3-6eb1-472e-863a-1fec4d3ec565'
      });

  <span id="freeText13574252508405903428" style="display:none">Anne is the mother of five, with never a dull moment in her lively home. And now, with a new baby on the way and insufferable Aunt Mary Maria visiting—and wearing out her welcome—Anne's life is full to bursting.<br /><br />Still, Mrs. Doctor can't think of any place she'd rather be than her own beloved Ingleside. Until the day she begins to worry that her adored Gilbert doesn't love her anymore. How could that be? She may be a little older, but she's still the same irrepressible, irreplaceable redhead—the wonderful Anne of Green Gables, all grown up. She's ready to make her cherished husband fall in love with her all over again!</span>

This question contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a href="/questions/475138-we-get-a-resolution-on-most-of-the-reasons"><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[We get a resolution on most of the reasons Anne feels that Gilbert doesn't love her anymore at the end of the book. But I still wonder- What resolution is there to the fact that Gilbert went to see his old girlfriend (and drug Anne along), not to mention that he did this on their anniversary?<br /><br />I really want to feel good about the ending of this book. Please help! :) <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></a>
</span>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/56993353-story-girl"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1577791645i/56993353._UY100_CR11,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/56993353-story-girl">Story_Girl</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    Christine was the sister of a friend of Gilbert who he was asked to keep an eye on well at collage, and she was engaged to someone back home. People s<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    Christine was the sister of a friend of Gilbert who he was asked to keep an eye on well at collage, and she was engaged to someone back home. People started rumors that they were going to announce their engagement, but as Anne had refused Gilbert, he decided he didn't care (he was too heartbroken to care what gossips said) and just let the rumors go. Anne believed the rumors right up until Gilbert asked her to marry him. So to Gilbert, he was merely vising an old friend he had not seen in a long time, not a girl friend, and as Miss Cornelia would say 'man-like', would never have given a second thought to this. But Anne still had little secret doubts about his relationship with Christine - this book finally ends those doubts for her. She will never again believe he had ever felt anything for Christine. I always look at it as tying up a lose end. And I think L.M. Montgomery chose to do so on an anniversary simply to add mood - Anne's fear and frustration - give it a little bit more of a basis. Hope this helps you feel better about the ending.<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>

<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I hadn't heard this, but I thought this book made Anne more believable as she deals with the struggle that enters every long-term relationship when th<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I hadn't heard this, but I thought this book made Anne more believable as she deals with the struggle that enters every long-term relationship when there are troublesome patches. It might not be as cozy, but it shows the strength and tenacity of true love.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

<div id="review_2211109406" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2211109406" />
    <a title="Miranda Reads" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/71848701-miranda-reads"><img alt="Miranda Reads" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1592243041p2/71848701.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2211109406?book_show_action=true">Jan 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Miranda Reads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Miranda Reads" href="/user/show/71848701-miranda-reads">Miranda Reads</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71848701-miranda-reads?shelf=literally-painful-to-read">literally-painful-to-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71848701-miranda-reads?shelf=mary-sue-i-will-kill-you">mary-sue-i-will-kill-you</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71848701-miranda-reads?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71848701-miranda-reads?shelf=2018-popsugar-reading-challenge">2018-popsugar-reading-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2211109406" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer896099386542219636"><b>Anne has become a secondary character....<i>in her own book</i></b>.<br/><br/><b>
  <i>So hopping mad right now.</i>
</b> Like she was literally shuffled off to the side in <b><u>ANNE</u> of Ingleside.</b> And what do we get is a book following her myriad of precocious (<i>read: annoying</i>) children. Seriously, every child is so pious, truthful, kind and considerate that it makes <b>me want to gag</b>.<br/><br/>I firmly believe that this is <b>not the same Anne</b> of Green Gables. She's been replaced body-snatcher style. <b>All personality and whimsy was ironed out,</b> and we </span>
  <span id="freeText896099386542219636" style="display:none"><b>Anne has become a secondary character....<i>in her own book</i></b>.<br /><br /><b>
  <i>So hopping mad right now.</i>
</b> Like she was literally shuffled off to the side in <b><u>ANNE</u> of Ingleside.</b> And what do we get is a book following her myriad of precocious (<i>read: annoying</i>) children. Seriously, every child is so pious, truthful, kind and considerate that it makes <b>me want to gag</b>.<br /><br />I firmly believe that this is <b>not the same Anne</b> of Green Gables. She's been replaced body-snatcher style. <b>All personality and whimsy was ironed out,</b> and we are left with this dull vaguely matronly woman with jealous tendencies.<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>"Do you really never feel that you want a broader life? You used to be quite ambitious, if I remember alright. Didn't you write some clever little things when you were at Redmond? A bit fantastic and whimsical, of course, but still..."</i>
</blockquote><br />As you may have guessed, I am not pleased with the way this series is turning out. We used to have this <b>crazy bold and brilliant girl</b>...and now we have this washed out mother. Instead of following Anne and her life, we get a series of almost-vignettes where each of her <b>six children</b> goes on an adventure and learns a life lesson.<br /><br />And yes, <b>some of the adventures are fun</b> - in particular, Rilla being absolutely mortified by the thought of having to <i>carry a cake</i> to the church was amusing (though, <b>reading her lisp was painful</b>). In addition, it was equally amusing to see Diana being tricked into thinking she was adopted. But at the same time, <b>I didn't really care for the children.</b> <br /><br />I read this story because I loved Anne, and we just <b>don't see her</b> all that much. When Anne does come into the story, she soothes the children, mildly scolds them or offers them sage wisdom. And that's it. It is like she's on horse tranquilizers. I will finish this series, but I have a feeling that I won't be too pleased with the next one...<br /><br /><i>The PopSugar 2018 Reading Challenge: The next book in a series you've started</i><br /><br /><a href="http://bit.ly/33ii2CT" rel="nofollow noopener">YouTube</a> | <a href="http://www.mirandareads.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Blog</a> | <a href="https://www.instagram.com/miranda.reads/?hl=en" rel="nofollow noopener">Instagram</a> | <a href="https://twitter.com/Miranda_Reads" rel="nofollow noopener">Twitter</a> | Snapchat @miranda.reads <br /><br />Happy Reading!</span>
  <a data-text-id="896099386542219636" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="treehugger" class="user" itemprop="url" name="treehugger" href="/user/show/123501-treehugger">treehugger</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/123501-treehugger?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer22706588" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17646304444863112692">Anne has disappeared by this point in the series and become a matronly woman with a brood of kids. The book focuses mainly on the trials and tribulations of her incredibly moral children and the town gossip, and we've lost some of the best characters like Rachel Lynde, Marilla, Davy, etc by this time as well.<br/><br/>I felt myself "hankering" after Avolea and the Anne that was before she married...<br/><br/>It just sort of dragged by with description after description of the seasons, the house, the nosy housekeepe</span>
  <span id="freeText17646304444863112692" style="display:none">Anne has disappeared by this point in the series and become a matronly woman with a brood of kids. The book focuses mainly on the trials and tribulations of her incredibly moral children and the town gossip, and we've lost some of the best characters like Rachel Lynde, Marilla, Davy, etc by this time as well.<br /><br />I felt myself "hankering" after Avolea and the Anne that was before she married...<br /><br />It just sort of dragged by with description after description of the seasons, the house, the nosy housekeeper...LMM just didn't keep Anne ANNE if you know what I mean. She's really no fun as a grown up, and as other commenters have mentioned, it is a huge disappointment that she gave up all of her writing when the children were born - and she has a live in handmaiden!! <br /><br />so, if you want to read about a redheaded woman respected by all as she wanders into middle age with her half-dozen kids and their gigantic house full of luxuries missing from most people in her village, have at it. <br /><br />But if you truly loved the Anne from Anne of Green Gables, perhaps you shouldn't spoil your opinion of her by watching her 'grow up'.<br /><br /></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2059678501" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2059678501" />
    <a title="Melki" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5293536-melki"><img alt="Melki" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1308071306p2/5293536.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2059678501?book_show_action=true">Jul 16, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melki" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melki" href="/user/show/5293536-melki">Melki</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/6145324-anne-of-ingleside">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=family-based-fiction">family-based-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=read-more-than-once">read-more-than-once</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2059678501" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13539907809629275968"><b>Anne laughed. "We never need to be economical in our imaginations, thank heaven."</b><br/><br/>Quite a few years have passed, and Anne is now the mother of five children, with another one on the way. Her life is full and busy, and, for the most part, happy. <i>This</i> is the book where Montgomery begins to shift her attention from Anne to Anne's tribe of children. Anne becomes little more than a peripheral character in the last two books in the series, as her exploits are now limited to her role as a wife and mothe</span>
  <span id="freeText13539907809629275968" style="display:none"><b>Anne laughed. "We never need to be economical in our imaginations, thank heaven."</b><br /><br />Quite a few years have passed, and Anne is now the mother of five children, with another one on the way. Her life is full and busy, and, for the most part, happy. <i>This</i> is the book where Montgomery begins to shift her attention from Anne to Anne's tribe of children. Anne becomes little more than a peripheral character in the last two books in the series, as her exploits are now limited to her role as a wife and mother; a necessary shift, true, but it provides less "scope for the imagination." But in the meantime, there's plenty to love in this go round. Anne's matchmaking skills yield unexpected results, and the children face various trials involving pets and friends. My favorite part was housekeeper Susan's schemes to rid the Blythes of a guest who <i>will not leave</i>:<br /><br /><b>"That woman came here in June and it is my opinion she means to stay here the rest of her life."</b><br /><br />You'll never guess what finally nudges the old bat out the door!<br /><br />The book ends with Anne making a nocturnal assessment of her sleeping family, admiring her children, and counting her blessings. It's a nice circle-of-life moment as we watch her exit center stage to take her place in the audience for the next chapter of a wonderful life story.<br /><br /><b>A very nice family, Anne reflected happily as she lay in her bed, with the baby beside her. Soon she would be about with them again, light-footed as of yore, loving them, teaching them, comforting them. They would be coming to her with their little joys and sorrows, their budding hopes, their new fears, their little problems that seemed so big to them and their little heartbreaks that seemed so bitter. She would hold all the threads of Ingleside life in her hands again to weave into a tapestry of beauty.</b></span>
  <a data-text-id="13539907809629275968" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2059678501">

<div id="review_2223522955" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2223522955" />
    <a title="E.F.B." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/39244832-e-f-b"><img alt="E.F.B." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1424876183p2/39244832.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2223522955?book_show_action=true">Jan 09, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="E.F.B." class="user" itemprop="url" name="E.F.B." href="/user/show/39244832-e-f-b">E.F.B.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39244832-e-f-b?shelf=childrens">childrens</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39244832-e-f-b?shelf=didn-t-hate-it-didn-t-love-it">didn-t-hate-it-didn-t-love-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39244832-e-f-b?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/39244832-e-f-b?shelf=classics">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2223522955" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15754806362229609105">3 stars = Didn't hate, but didn't fall in love like with most of the previous books. <br/><br/>This was probably my least favorite book in the Anne series so far. Usually the positives in these books outweigh the negatives for me, but in this one they were about half and half. I’ll cover the negative first, and then follow up with the positives so that I end my review on a good note, because that’s how I prefer to end things.<br/><br/><b>Negatives: </b><br/><br/>For one thing, I found it jarring that 7 or 9 years (I was really conf</span>
  <span id="freeText15754806362229609105" style="display:none">3 stars = Didn't hate, but didn't fall in love like with most of the previous books. <br /><br />This was probably my least favorite book in the Anne series so far. Usually the positives in these books outweigh the negatives for me, but in this one they were about half and half. I’ll cover the negative first, and then follow up with the positives so that I end my review on a good note, because that’s how I prefer to end things.<br /><br /><b>Negatives: </b><br /><br />For one thing, I found it jarring that 7 or 9 years (I was really confused which it was) had passed between the previous book and this one. Last I saw Anne she was in her mid-twenties with one baby, then BOOM she's mid-thirties with five kids and another on the way. I understand why Montgomery would have wanted to jump forward like that for the sake of being able to write about the kids and their lives, but it was also just making me feel like, "WHOA pump the breaks, let's slow down a little here!" Once I got used to the new time frame it didn't bother me as much, but it just wasn't the somewhat smooth transition to the new book I'd been used to. Plus, all of Anne and Diana's reminiscing about yesteryear at the very beginning of the book made me feel a bit nostalgic in a sad kind of way, partially because they were kind of talking like being thirty something is SO OLD and the best part of their lives was their childhood, which already passed. And I'm like, "Um, NO. The best part of your lives is, or at least can be, RIGHT NOW if you CHOOSE TO MAKE IT THAT WAY by appreciating all the things and people you’ve been blessed with and making the most of EVERY DAY and EVERY BREATH you’re given." <br /><br />I mean, I understand that they miss the freedom they had as children and being able to spend time together the way they used to. I also don’t have a problem with looking back at the past and remembering where you’ve been so you can learn from it and better appreciate where you are now. But I think there’s a delicate balance between visiting the past and wishing you were still living in it, the latter of which is not, in my opinion, the attitude God wants us to have. Thankfully though, the story did move on (I was a little afraid this sad nostalgia would be a theme through the whole book) and it started to be apparent that Anne actually is content in her current life and is happy, which made be feel better. I just didn’t like feeling the sad nostalgia right at the beginning of the book.<br /><br />Another thing that landed on the negative side for me was that, usually, I enjoy the colorful personalities of the secondary characters in the Anne books. They tend to be so outrageous that it makes me laugh (which I believe is the author’s intention). In this one, however, some of the outrageous personalities struck me as more annoying than funny. I literally kept saying “good grief!” to myself over an over because, good grief, who acts like that?! (Sadly, there are people in RL who act like that, but still!) Aunt Mary Maria was one of the big ones. I hated her negative, controlling, manipulative attitude, especially the fact that she, who didn’t have kids, thought she knew so much better than Anne and Gilbert how to raise the Ingleside kids, and even slapped one of the girls at one point. In my book, you do not slap a child in the face EVER. No. Nada. I cannot tolerate this, and the fact that it wasn’t even her own child only made me more angry. It also made me mad that Anne, Gilbert, and Susan couldn’t seem to stand up to her just because she was family and she acted like a victim every time they tried. I’m like, HECK NO. There are some lines you don’t cross, I don’t care how lonely you are living by yourself. Emotional manipulation and physical abuse of my children, my husband, my staff, and myself is the line. You crossed it. GET. OUT. OF. MY. HOUSE. *fume*<br /><br />The behavior of some of the non-Ingleside children on the island also drove me a bit bonkers. I’m not even saying that it was not realistic—I remember my own childhood peers doing and saying similar things at right around the same ages as these characters (and it annoyed me just as much in real life as in fiction)—it just seemed like the ones that behaved badly got quite a bit of attention in this book. Like, we’re told that the Ingleside children have had some good friends, but we’re only TOLD about them, we do not SEE them. What we do see is super obnoxious kids lying to, bullying, threatening, and generally treating the Ingleside kids badly. I could have tolerated a couple examples of this because goodness knows kids are not always kind to each other, but I would have so much preferred the focus to be on the good friendships and seen the Ingleside children find their own kindred spirits like Diana was to Anne.<br /><br />I also felt like there was way more skippable "flab" in this book than in any of the previous books. I have skipped stuff that just went on too long in previous books, but it was usually only once or twice per-book that I had to do it. With this one, on the other hand, especially in the latter half of the book, there were at least four or five instances where things were going too long, and some of it was inconsequential to anything, the section of story featuring the quilting circle ladies being the worst offender. Then there were some things that were consequential, but just went on and on and on until I was like, “I understood the point of this mini-story three page ago! Can we just get a conclusion already???”) So, I started doing what I usually don’t like doing in most books and skipping pages, sometimes upward of four or more pages at a time, and was able to do this without feeling like I’d missed anything of importance. <br /><br />My final gripe is a place in the final chapter where I felt like the book actually disagreed with the back-cover synopsis in a disappointing way. The back cover of my copy says, “…[Anne] begins to worry that her adored Gilbert doesn’t love her anymore. […] She’s ready to make her cherished husband fall in love with her all over again!” I took this to mean that Anne was going to reach a place where she felt her marriage was getting stale (which did happen) and then was going to be the determined Anne we know and love and <b>take action </b> to put some excitement back into their marriage and it would be all romantic and cute and stuff (which didn’t happen). What actually happened was that Anne got stuck in one of those annoying ruts in life I know we all get stuck in at some point where it feels like you’re just doing the same thing day in and day out, and nothing will ever change, and you don’t know how to change it, and you get frustrated and bitter and grumpy and just in a really negative, unhealthy headspace. Added on to this was the fact that Gilbert truly seemed “out of it”, like he didn’t notice anything Anne did anymore, didn’t call her by the endearing nicknames, kissed her like it was just habit and didn’t mean anything, etc. I was waiting for Anne to get determined and take that action the back cover had promised, but she just…didn’t. What she did do was get more mad and frustrated, and decided that if Gilbert was tired of her, fine, she’d be all jealous and sad and wouldn’t even remind him of their anniversary when it came around if he was going to forget it, and basically spiraled into this pit of bitterness and self-pity until something happened (something she took no action to cause) that changed everything and made her realize how wrong she’d been to be thinking the way that she was. Realizing your mistake and learning from it and apologizing for your behavior: Good. Deciding to get mad and stay mad instead of…oh, I don’t know…COMMUNICATING WITH YOUR HUSBAND AND MAYBE EVEN ASKING HIM WHY HE’S BEEN SO OUT OF IT LATELY, WHICH WOULD HAVE ANSWERED ALL YOUR QUESTIONS AND SOLVED THE PROBLEM: <b>BAD</b> Of course, I can’t put all the blame on Anne in this case. The reason Gilbert had been so out of it for two weeks was because of intense job-related stress which he thought he told Anne about, but didn’t. I just…*facepalm* Really, Gilbert, really? I generally like you as Anne’s husband, so please don’t make me have to reach through the book, grab you by the collar, and give you a good talking to about women not being mind-readers!!! Well, thankfully it seems they both learned their lesson about communication and I hope I won’t have to be irritated by them in this manner again.<br /><br />/end super ranty rant about the negatives of this book <br /><br /><br /><b>Positives: </b><br /><br />Now for the good stuff! Among the things I liked about this book, I really (minus the gripes I mentioned above in relation to the aunt-who-shall-not-be-named^) loved Anne and Gilbert as parents, and especially appreciated the way Anne handled her children’s problems: By letting them say what they needed to say without judgement or interruption, taking their feelings and their problems seriously even if they seemed silly and superfluous to her as an adult, and then simply explaining the truth to them in a matter-of-fact, non-condescending way. That’s good parenting, folks, and actually a really good way to treat kids in general. *thumbs up* <br /><br />And, minus the issue at the end which was caused by lack of communication, I liked Anne and Gilbert as husband and wife in that they do actively strive to keep their marriage healthy and show genuine love to each other, unlike so many of their peers who married for convenience and let themselves reach a point where they resented their spouse and seemed to think that was just the natural course of life and marriage. (hint: IT’S NOT!)<br /><br />I also liked pretty much all of the Ingleside kids. Yes, they made mistakes sometimes, but the majority of these mistakes were of the entertaining variety caused by the natural immaturity and naivety of childhood, not the obnoxious, annoying, variety, like with the other kids on the island who seemed to get a kick out of lying and drowning puppies and kittens. *eye twitch* In general, the Ingleside kids seemed to be genuinely sweet kids whom I look forward getting to know better in the last two books.<br /><br />I’m somewhat surprised to say that this may also be the one book in this series that didn’t make me cry my eyes out at any point. I was braced for it because it seems like Montgomery has managed to pull tears out of me once in every book up till now, and I actually did spot the thing I think was supposed to be the Sad Event of this book, but… It just didn’t make me cry. I think the reason was A) I saw it coming pretty much as soon as that section of the story started and B) this “individual” wasn’t even on the page all that long and I didn’t have that much reason to get emotionally attached to them. So… Not crying for once was actually somewhat refreshing. :p<br /><br />And finally, Montgomery was once again faithful in providing a satisfying ending. It wasn’t so satisfying that I wanted to hug the book like with the previous one, but it was still a pleasant way for it to end (except for that tiny touch of foreshadowing about Walter’s future, WAH!!! ._. ) and when I put the book down, I was content. <br /><br />Like I said at the start, I didn’t hate this book, but there was enough that annoyed me that I never fell in love either. That does not, however, mean I didn’t enjoy any of it, so I’m giving it a neutral 3 stars and move right along to the next book.<br /><br /><b>Content Advisory:</b><br /><br /><b>Sexual content:</b> Mild hints of romance here and there. Super vague hints at Anne’s pregnancy. A mention of someone kissing someone else absently as if it has become habit and no longer means anything.<br /><br /><b>Violence: </b> One instance where we’re told of a child being slapped in the face by an adult. A couple mentions of dogs dying early deaths because they ate an unknown poisonous substance (we do not “see” the deaths, we only know that the dogs went downhill until they died.) Two suggestions of child abuse, one from a child who turns out to be a chronic liar, and one where it sounds like it might actually be true because the child’s father is known as a drunkard. (We never “see” a child being abused.) Kids threaten each other verbally and physically, but no one is ever seriously hurt. <br /><br /><b>Other negatives:</b> Many of the kids on the island (with the exception of the Ingleside kids, who we’re told are not lied to at home and therefore don’t expect to be lied to elsewhere and don’t have a habit of lying themselves) lie to each other and the Ingleside kids pretty frequently. Some of the lies are just exaggeration, but some of them are pretty cruel, including, but not limited to, telling a young boy who is away from home that he was sent away because his mother is dying, and in fact, is probably already dead. The kids who tell this lie have no sympathy when the boy becomes visibly upset and seem to enjoy upsetting him. <br /><br />Children also badmouth each other and other children’s parents.<br /><br />Women shamelessly gossip during a quilting circle and only seem to regret it when they realize a child was spying on them and have to think back to see if they said anything a child shouldn’t hear.<br /><br />One episodic story is in the form of a memory of something that happened at a funeral many years ago, that everyone seems to think was pretty terrible and supposedly isn’t a story for children. (Turns out the man who died was a pretty mean person when he was alive, and the sister of his widow crashes the funeral and tells everyone the truth about how badly he treated everyone, including his wife. This woman has grown so bitter she says she’s going to laugh at the man’s dead body, but then has to leave because she’s weeping too hard. Another woman at the funeral who is apparently even more bitter than her, thanks her for telling everyone the truth.<br /><br /><b>Worldviews:</b> Anne and Gilbert seem to be Christians: They go to church, believe in God, pray, quote Scripture, and raise their children in these beliefs. Sometimes the kids get ideas in their heads about God that are not biblical, but they are later corrected in a loving way.<br /><br />As in all of the Anne books, there are some side characters who claim to be “Christian” but who do not behave as such. <br /><br />In one episodic story a child’s father is said to be an atheist, and that child later tells one of the Ingleside girls that God isn’t real. This upsets the girl deeply, but when she gets home, Anne lets her talk about it the same way she lets all the kids talk about things they’re upset about, and the girl decides she still believes in God.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15754806362229609105" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText3797824937538083241" style="display:none"><b>Full (mini) review now posted!</b><br /><br />It’s fun to watch someone grow from a child into an adult with children of their own. Anne of Green Gables is now Anne of Ingleside, and her home is filled with life and laughter. She is a wife settled into her marriage, and is now the mother of a whole passel of children. This group of kids is incredibly varied in looks and personality, but they’re all children of imagination and character, like their parents. This book is mainly a collection of tales in the children’s lives, though there are still stories from Anne’s perspective, as well. It honestly felt more like a collection short stories than an actual novel, but it was still very enjoyable. Every story was entertaining and funny. Also, this is the first legitimately new to me book in this series. I had read much more of it than I had originally thought, but not this one!</span>

<div id="review_168033519" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/168033519" />
    <a title="Rachel M" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2916975-rachel-m"><img alt="Rachel M" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1257724097p2/2916975.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/168033519?book_show_action=true">May 13, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rachel M" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rachel M" href="/user/show/2916975-rachel-m">Rachel M</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer168033519" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15999013688376643491"> Reading this book after so many years is like visiting a childhood haunt after many years to find only that it was not so big, not so beautiful, not so miraculous, not so mysterious, as it was then. It is like spending your early life thinking your parents are the height of perfection, compared to the day they begin to have visible (sometimes glaring) flaws. I know that this all comes full-circle in adulthood and that the day returns when you look again at your parents, at the old childhood hau</span>
  <span id="freeText15999013688376643491" style="display:none"> Reading this book after so many years is like visiting a childhood haunt after many years to find only that it was not so big, not so beautiful, not so miraculous, not so mysterious, as it was then. It is like spending your early life thinking your parents are the height of perfection, compared to the day they begin to have visible (sometimes glaring) flaws. I know that this all comes full-circle in adulthood and that the day returns when you look again at your parents, at the old childhood haunt, in wonder and gratefulness. But as a reader of Anne of Green Gables, I am afraid I am smack-dab in the teenage years. <br /> <br /> Then: no one said anything better than L.M. Montgomery. No one saw what she did in nature, no one understood people the way she did, no one understood me better than she did. That was the world I wanted to live in, Prince Edward Island, nature and small society, fully equipped with imagination.<br /> <br /> Now: I am galled by the selfish prejudices. The only people worth knowing are the imaginitive ones, but what gets so annoying is that they all have the same imagination. Every single protagonist she ever writes of personifies a tree, has the same tastes (sometimes even so specifically as what thickness bread should be); each faces the defeat of mistaken dreams, each carries a certain pride about themselves, and each one is spiteful towards the exact same types of personalities. As a child I loved L.M. Montgomery's opinions - they were my own. Her characters' experiences of being dreamy, imaginitive and often ridiculed for it were my experiences. She seemed to get it. Now I want other things in a writer. I want the writer to see more than I see in human nature, not less. I don't want a writer only to see my point of view - I recognize that I need more than just the same ways of seeing the world. Was Montgomery capable of writing other than of herself? <br /><br /> Part of why I feel frustration in revisiting the Anne-world again is that it used to fit so well, and now it no longer does. It's like the perfect pair of jeans you had at age 14 or that party dress from the 80s that was your favorite. You want to go back and feel the times past as they felt then, and belong to a world that felt just about perfect when you were there. Or maybe we go back in our memories to the old places and the old times because what we really want is a perfect place of peace, happiness, security, and they were closest to us as children. </span>
  <a data-text-id="15999013688376643491" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Manybooks" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Manybooks" href="/user/show/2970379-manybooks">Manybooks</a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=childrens-literature">childrens-literature</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2970379-manybooks?shelf=book-reviews">book-reviews</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer78646052" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4575410855163961389">Albeit there is nothing that I would consider actually terribly "wrong" with L.M. Montgomery's <u>Anne of Ingleside</u>, I also have never really managed to enjoy this particular <i>Anne of Green Gables</i> story all that much. And I guess that my lack of personal reading pleasure stems mostly from the fact that there just is not nearly enough Anne Blythe (Anne Shirley) in <u>Anne of Ingleside</u> and conversely rather too much information and details not only about Anne's children (which I actually have not found a</span>
  <span id="freeText4575410855163961389" style="display:none">Albeit there is nothing that I would consider actually terribly "wrong" with L.M. Montgomery's <u>Anne of Ingleside</u>, I also have never really managed to enjoy this particular <i>Anne of Green Gables</i> story all that much. And I guess that my lack of personal reading pleasure stems mostly from the fact that there just is not nearly enough Anne Blythe (Anne Shirley) in <u>Anne of Ingleside</u> and conversely rather too much information and details not only about Anne's children (which I actually have not found all that frustrating in and of itself, although I do kind of wish that Montgomery had not gone so out of her way to make Walter Blythe appear as strange, uncanny and not meant for this world) but also and considerably more frustratingly and infuriatingly simply way too much about the Blythe Family housekeeper Susan Baker (whom I absolutely DESPISE with every fibre of my being and find at best annoying and for the most part generally quite as grating on my nerves and temperament as fingernails being repeatedly scraped down a chalkboard). <br /><br />But furthermore, even the sadly far too few times where Anne does play a more solid and active role in <u>Anne of Ingleside</u>, I have found her either too much into gossiping and matchmaking or for no good reason feeling totally insecure about Gilbert possibly not loving her anymore (with that whole episode of her jealously of Christine Stuart really and majorly annoying me, as the entire scenario just seems to be rather tacked on by L.M. Montgomery, almost as though she did not have enough decent material to provide a realistic ending for <u>Anne of Ingleside</u>). As while the Christine Stuart episode and Anne's doubt regarding Gilbert's love for her are not terrible and inappropriate in and of themselves, they do not really feel like a natural continuation and progression of the storylines of <u>Anne of Ingleside</u>, as I for one have always felt that Anne's doubts and her feeling that her husband might not love her anymore do seem to come right out of the blue so to speak and this does indeed make <u>Anne of Ingleside</u> rather disjointed as a story (and yes, I also do have to somewhat wonder whether the fact that <u>Anne of Ingleside</u> was actually the last story of the <i>Anne of Green Gables</i> series to be published has anything to do with this, as perhaps L.M. Montgomery was in 1939 not only really sick and tired of writing about Anne and company but also probably just wanted to be over and done with the series and was therefore perhaps rushing to finish and not really caring all that much anymore if the ending she had chosen even made all that much sense for the kind of character Anne Shirley/Anne Blythe had always been portrayed in the books, in the <i>Anne of Green Gables</i> novels).</span>
  <a data-text-id="4575410855163961389" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/123564494" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/123564494?book_show_action=true">Sep 26, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kate" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kate" href="/user/show/253354-kate">Kate</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer123564494" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer420451426756045193">I set out to read the full Anne of Green Gables series (I bought the set at a yard sale). But this one was the last I could take for a while. The author has difficulty making it clear who her audience is. Is it children who want stories about other children, or is it people who have grown up with Anne and want to know more about her ongoing life? As the book begins, it seems to be the latter audience, but it soon becomes clear it's the former. She starts this book in Avonlea, and we see Anne's c</span>
  <span id="freeText420451426756045193" style="display:none">I set out to read the full Anne of Green Gables series (I bought the set at a yard sale). But this one was the last I could take for a while. The author has difficulty making it clear who her audience is. Is it children who want stories about other children, or is it people who have grown up with Anne and want to know more about her ongoing life? As the book begins, it seems to be the latter audience, but it soon becomes clear it's the former. She starts this book in Avonlea, and we see Anne's childhood friend Diana. But we hear nothing of how Marilla, Davy, Dora, and other loved ones fare, in a book that covers six or seven years. This is disappointing. The anecdotes about Anne's children are moderately entertaining, but perhaps not as much so as stories in the other novels. The previous book, Anne's House of Dreams, has an actual plot line to follow, but this book rambles on forever about nothing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="420451426756045193" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_243698384" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/243698384" />
    <a title="Rosalba" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/7120149-rosalba"><img alt="Rosalba" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1325900759p2/7120149.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/243698384?book_show_action=true">Dec 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Rosalba" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Rosalba" href="/user/show/7120149-rosalba">Rosalba</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7120149-rosalba?shelf=lm-montgomery">lm-montgomery</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer243698384" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9004476951069900196">This is the last book in the Anne of Green Gables series that still feels like an Anne book. The next two, are really more about Anne´s children and their playmates, the Merediths.<br/>Anne´s children are wonderful, but I still love her best. Some friends have expressed dissapointed at finding Anne grown up and "settled down". I don´t. Married life, with its ups and downs, is still as filled with adventure as childhood, only now the adventures are different.I believe the Ingleside children's stories </span>
  <span id="freeText9004476951069900196" style="display:none">This is the last book in the Anne of Green Gables series that still feels like an Anne book. The next two, are really more about Anne´s children and their playmates, the Merediths.<br />Anne´s children are wonderful, but I still love her best. Some friends have expressed dissapointed at finding Anne grown up and "settled down". I don´t. Married life, with its ups and downs, is still as filled with adventure as childhood, only now the adventures are different.I believe the Ingleside children's stories were woven in among Anne´s because a Mother always lives her children's adventures along with them. And at the end of each Ingleside story, there was always Anne, comforting the little lad or maiden whose turn it was in the spotlight.<br /><br />Like all the Anne books, this one left me wriggling with joy as I reread those passages I know almost by heart. I loved the first and last chapters- the picnic with Diana remembering old days was wonderful, there I could still see the dear fanciful Anne I love and that vanishes unexpectedly in the last two books. And the last chapter where Anne is depressed and jealous of Cristine Stuart is an interesting choice for the last Anne story L.M. Montgomery ever published. Anne´s bitterness cuts the heart- I can imagine L.M. Montgomery projected her own feelings onto her beloved character- but the unfolding is as beautiful as could ever be expected.<br /> All the in-between Ingleside children stories were sweet, some were delightful, and others just a little flat. My favorites were the sewing circle with Walter listening, Jem´s incident with the pearl collar, and Nan's two accounts where her imagination just got over the top: the switched at birth incident and the one with the Misterious Lady.Although I wished we could have stayed a bit longer in Avonlea and taken a look at all the character we know from earlier books, like Marilla, Davy, Paul.And I also noticed Shirley never got one adventure of his own. All the other did, even five-year-old Rilla.<br />SPOILER AHEAD!!!! DONT READ IF YOU HAVENT READ RILLA OF INGLESIDE!!!!!!!<br /><br />I was surprised by the many references to Walter's early death in the war- I counted at least three-that I hadn't noticed before. You see, I read Anne of Ingleside before Rilla of Ingleside and didn´t get a clue about what was to come.<br /><br />END OF SPOILER <br /><br /><br />Overall, this is a delicious read I highly reccommend to everyone. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="9004476951069900196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_442809350" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/442809350" />
    <a title="Alexandra" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5809659-alexandra"><img alt="Alexandra" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1476168545p2/5809659.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/442809350?book_show_action=true">Oct 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alexandra" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alexandra" href="/user/show/5809659-alexandra">Alexandra</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5809659-alexandra?shelf=canadian">canadian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5809659-alexandra?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5809659-alexandra?shelf=early-20th-century">early-20th-century</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5809659-alexandra?shelf=2012">2012</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer442809350" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16591046688939737261">Where is Anne of Green Gables, and who is this dull matron with no apparent inner life?<br/><br/>This book was a slog, but it's hardly surprising, since, in my understanding, Montgomery was plagued by her publishers for more Anne crapola, and this was the result. Though her descriptive passages of view and season are stunning as ever, that's about all that can be said for this work.<br/><br/>I am rather troubled by a tendency of reviewers here to criticize the character of Anne by railing against their perception o</span>
  <span id="freeText16591046688939737261" style="display:none">Where is Anne of Green Gables, and who is this dull matron with no apparent inner life?<br /><br />This book was a slog, but it's hardly surprising, since, in my understanding, Montgomery was plagued by her publishers for more Anne crapola, and this was the result. Though her descriptive passages of view and season are stunning as ever, that's about all that can be said for this work.<br /><br />I am rather troubled by a tendency of reviewers here to criticize the character of Anne by railing against their perception of how poorly she performs her gender role without putting it into historical context. Sure, she's a shitty mother by the standards of anyone who wasn't raised by maids with silver spoons and all that, but in Montgomery's day, this young woman was a top performer in the motherhood-as-profession finals. The bald claim that Anne is a bad mother because she doesn't slave all day in her kitchen is a troubling approach that tells nothing about the book, and a whole lot about the reviewer.<br /><br />I was, however, as annoyed as other reviewers by all the missed opportunities for useful social commentary by Montgomery, which would have been much preferred to the frequent descents into base stereotyping (especially of the lower class), but at least she didn't start in on the French this time, so I suppose I should count my blessings. And, again, context. This is a book with no pretence of masterwork-hood.<br /><br />I want to read the whole series for no reason other than completism at this point in what has proven an increasingly painful journey, (well, that and slowly watching social change happen in the background as the series goes on). But I don't know how much more molding-barbaric-children-into-earnest-Christians I can stomach. The next book in the series may require a higher booze intake, since it's all about the little buggers.<br /><br />Don't read this book. Or the reviews. Just turn around and walk away, and everything will be fine.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16591046688939737261" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText13702064331839353380" style="display:none">Oh, Anne! Anne!! I feel as if you've changed! You're not quite the same girl you were. You still have an occasional imagining, but you're just too sensible now. I like it so much better when practicality didn't get in your way. <br /><br />Aside from this horrible change, I still thoroughly enjoyed this book. It's not my favorite, but it still brightens any day and makes me laugh! <br /><br />It was wonderful to get to know Anne's children. Although, not one of them captures that same personality as Anne possessed as a child, and even adult. <br /><br />Nan and Di's mishaps were perhaps, the highlights of the book! They were just too funny. Never trust anyone who is bragging that she's been 'nearly killed' countless times! <br /><br />Walter, you are my favorite Blythe child. You're soul is old, though you are young. You're not a sissy! I don't care what any of those other children says! You're just wonderful, and sweet and so much like your mother!<br /><br />Rilla, the darling, little, chubby baby! I do believe you are somewhat of a brat for throwing that cake in the creek, river, whatever. Pure and simple bratty-ness! But, let's admit, cute and hilarious, not matter how bratty! <br /><br />Jem, you tug at my heart! There's just something, some air, about you that makes me feel so sorry for you! You're not like the other Blythe children. Maybe because you were born in the House of Dreams! Anyway, you go off and fight pirates and hunt tigers, if you so wish! <br /><br />Shirley, I hate to say that I have nothing to say about you. You weren't really mentioned too awfully much in this book. <br /><br />Aside from the fact that I felt Anne growing distant in this one, I do really, truly love this book!</span>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer547047885" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4156504508328474887">The last Anne book... I had been saving it, treasuring it, for when I felt down. The day came recently when in low spirits I cracked into the last part of this happy story. It helped, it is a cheerful book no doubt. None of this tired "the world will never be the same..." business. Unfortunately Anne barely plays a role in the sixth installment. The book is filled with short stories about her children and seasons that come and go all too quickly. The writing is still very sweet, and there are oc</span>
  <span id="freeText4156504508328474887" style="display:none">The last Anne book... I had been saving it, treasuring it, for when I felt down. The day came recently when in low spirits I cracked into the last part of this happy story. It helped, it is a cheerful book no doubt. None of this tired "the world will never be the same..." business. Unfortunately Anne barely plays a role in the sixth installment. The book is filled with short stories about her children and seasons that come and go all too quickly. The writing is still very sweet, and there are occasional sections of that sublime nature conjuration. But it may as well not be under the Anne title. In my personal world Anne of Green Gables is a 5 book affair.<br /><br />Throughout the entire series I was waiting eagerly for some expression on Anne's part of her love and gratitude toward Marilla. I am so exasperated that this never occurred. Yes, I can say to myself that Marilla's influence was too great, too obvious, for Anne to even attempt to express in words. That is the only way I can cope really. Still it is sad that Anne never acknowledges Marilla as her mother.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4156504508328474887" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Krista" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10031062-krista"><img alt="Krista" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558467451p2/10031062.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2126148357?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Krista" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Krista" href="/user/show/10031062-krista">Krista</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback " href="/book/show/17379244-anne-of-ingleside">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10031062-krista?shelf=2017">2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2126148357" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15126734870396190186">Still just as charming as ever. However, there were a few parts that just seemed to drag on and on. I found myself skimming during the quilting bee when all the women were sharing gossip about a bunch of people I didn't know or care to know. The stories of the adventures of Anne's children were the stars of the show with this book. </span>

          </span>

<div id="review_3148722806" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3148722806" />
    <a title="Abigail" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/87835420-abigail"><img alt="Abigail" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539121384p2/87835420.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3148722806?book_show_action=true">Jan 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abigail" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abigail" href="/user/show/87835420-abigail">Abigail</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover " href="/book/show/3629346-anne-of-ingleside">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Readers Who Enjoyed the First Five 'Anne' Books</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=childrens-fiction">childrens-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=vintage-girls">vintage-girls</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=family-stories">family-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=orphans">orphans</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=sweet-youth">sweet-youth</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=lm-montgomery">lm-montgomery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/87835420-abigail?shelf=canadian-children">canadian-children</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3148722806" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10170009586024742696">Although currently considered the sixth entry in Canadian author L.M. Montgomery's classic series of children's novels about red-headed heroine Anne Shirley - the first (and most famous) volume, <u>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8127.Anne_of_Green_Gables__Anne_of_Green_Gables___1_' title='Anne of Green Gables (Anne of Green Gables, #1) by L.M. Montgomery' rel='nofollow noopener'>Anne of Green Gables</a>
</u>, was released in 1908, while the eighth and final one, <u>
  <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/433533.Rilla_of_Ingleside__Anne_of_Green_Gables___8_' title='Rilla of Ingleside (Anne of Green Gables, #8) by L.M. Montgomery' rel='nofollow noopener'>Rilla of Ingleside</a>
</u>, appeared in 1921 - <u>Anne of Ingleside</u> was actually published in 1939, long after its companions. It was, in fact, the last of Montgomery's works to appear in her lifetime, making it into print just thre</span>
  <span id="freeText10170009586024742696" style="display:none">Although currently considered the sixth entry in Canadian author L.M. Montgomery's classic series of children's novels about red-headed heroine Anne Shirley - the first (and most famous) volume, <u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8127.Anne_of_Green_Gables__Anne_of_Green_Gables___1_" title="Anne of Green Gables (Anne of Green Gables, #1) by L.M. Montgomery" rel="nofollow noopener">Anne of Green Gables</a>
</u>, was released in 1908, while the eighth and final one, <u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/433533.Rilla_of_Ingleside__Anne_of_Green_Gables___8_" title="Rilla of Ingleside (Anne of Green Gables, #8) by L.M. Montgomery" rel="nofollow noopener">Rilla of Ingleside</a>
</u>, appeared in 1921 - <u>Anne of Ingleside</u> was actually published in 1939, long after its companions. It was, in fact, the last of Montgomery's works to appear in her lifetime, making it into print just three years before her death in 1942. I was very conscious of that fact, and of the recent revelations about Montgomery's likely suicide, during my rereading of the book, undertaken for a group discussion over in <i>The L.M. Montgomery Book Club</i> to which I belong, and was especially struck by some of the more melancholy passages that appear in its pages.<br /><br />Meant to fill in gaps between <u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77394.Anne_s_House_of_Dreams__Anne_of_Green_Gables___5_" title="Anne's House of Dreams (Anne of Green Gables, #5) by L.M. Montgomery" rel="nofollow noopener">Anne's House of Dreams</a>
</u> (1917), which chronicles the first year of Anne's married life with Gilbert Blythe, and <u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/77395.Rainbow_Valley__Anne_of_Green_Gables___7_" title="Rainbow Valley (Anne of Green Gables, #7) by L.M. Montgomery" rel="nofollow noopener">Rainbow Valley</a>
</u> (1919), which focuses almost entirely upon the six Blythe children, the narrative of <u>Anne of Ingleside</u> is divided between Anne and her children, sometimes chronicling the former's trials and tribulations, as when she comes to doubt Gilbert's regard for her, toward the end of the book; and sometimes featuring the children's adventures and misadventures, from Jem's dog-related sorrows to Di's string of false friends. The result is a book that feels, much like the epistolary <u>
  <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/65840.Anne_of_Windy_Poplars__Anne_of_Green_Gables___4_" title="Anne of Windy Poplars (Anne of Green Gables, #4) by L.M. Montgomery" rel="nofollow noopener">Anne of Windy Poplars</a>
</u>, rather episodic. I found it quite charming, for all that, and while I'm not entirely sure it succeeds as a novel, enjoyed many of the individual episodes enough that it didn't make much difference to me. Anne's reunion with her childhood friend, Diana, and their day of remembrances of times past; the visit of the deliciously obnoxious Aunt Mary Maria Blythe to Ingleside, and the unexpected cause of her departure; the birth of little Rilla, and Walter's anguish, when exiled from home that day; the poignant discovery, on Jem's part, that you can buy a dog, but not his love - these episodes all appealed to me immensely, even if others - Nan's castle-in-the-air, regarding the GLOOMY HOUSE, for instance - strained my suspension of disbelief.<br /><br /><u>Anne of Ingleside</u> is, in my estimation, the weakest of the Anne books, and despite my enjoyment, I am always cognizant of its flaws. There are some classist undercurrents here - the almost gleefully detailed descriptions of the poorer houses visited by the children, from Jenny Penny's run-down home, with its noisy, crowded dinner table, to the filthy seaside shanty of six-toed Jimmy Thomas - that I find rather unpleasant, and Anne herself sometimes appears as a distant figure, implausibly perfect as a mother, and curiously inactive, compared to her younger days. That said, I do think that the positives outweigh the negatives, and the realization that this was the last of Montgomery's books - something I had not been aware of, when reading it previously - gave the reading experience added interest and poignancy. When Anne describes herself as <i>"a creature in an nightmare, trying to overtake someone with fettered feet,"</i> or laments that <i>"Nothing had any meaning any longer. Everything seemed remote and unreal,"</i> one wonders whether Montgomery was writing from her own experience, at the moment of composition.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10170009586024742696" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_60352496" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/60352496" />
    <a title="Melissa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1507563-melissa"><img alt="Melissa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1494359880p2/1507563.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/60352496?book_show_action=true">Jun 19, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melissa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melissa" href="/user/show/1507563-melissa">Melissa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer60352496" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9087433876053349852">So, after reading the Anne of Green Gables, first 3 books for book club I decided I wanted to read some of the later books in the series. I have enjoyed them. I think Anne's House of Dreams is my favorite. I'm currently on the last of the "Anne" titled books and there is something that is funny/frustrating to me.<br/><br/>Anne and Diana are talking and it mentions how Diana is 'matronly' looking i.e. chubby. (Now remember this is like 15 years later, so the girls are 30-ish.) And how Diana has always been</span>
  <span id="freeText9087433876053349852" style="display:none">So, after reading the Anne of Green Gables, first 3 books for book club I decided I wanted to read some of the later books in the series. I have enjoyed them. I think Anne's House of Dreams is my favorite. I'm currently on the last of the "Anne" titled books and there is something that is funny/frustrating to me.<br /><br />Anne and Diana are talking and it mentions how Diana is 'matronly' looking i.e. chubby. (Now remember this is like 15 years later, so the girls are 30-ish.) And how Diana has always been not-thin etc. Also both Anne and Diana have now had SEVERAL children.<br /><br />Anne and Diana are spending the day together remembering their youth.<br /><br />"Don't talk as if 'our day' were ended, Diana. We're only fifteen years old and kindred spirits. The air isn't just full of light...it is light. I'm not sure that I haven't sprouted wings."<br />"I feel just that way, too." said Diana, forgetting that she had tipped the scale at one hundred and fifty-five that morning.<br /><br />It then goes on to talk about how Diana kept eating 'slivers' of chocolate cake. And finally Diana ... "picked up the plate which held the remainder of the chocolate cake...looked at it longingly...shook her head and packed it in the basket with an expression of great nobility and self-denial on her face.<br /><br />Granted - this book was written a long time ago, and obviously people were skinner then. I guess I am just frustrated to have such a negative picture of being 155. Of course this may have hit a nerve with me since I am trying to loose weight after having Josh, but still, I would love to be 155. Aargh!<br /><br />Also strangely in this series there is very little mention of pregnancy. Even among people talking to each other. When the first baby was due in House of Dreams it talked about how Anne was looking forward to a 'much expected time'. And she would be 'busy during the fall." But never came out and talked about what was going on. Anne and Gilbert didn't even tell their kids when Anne was going to have another, just sent them all to neighbors when the baby was due. How could this happen? I wonder if this was just because the books were written for children, or was life really like that with no mention of pregnancy except a 'delicate issue' here or there? Weird.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9087433876053349852" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Elinor  Loredan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2504970-elinor-loredan"><img alt="Elinor  Loredan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1416182317p2/2504970.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/131067789?book_show_action=true">Nov 16, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elinor  Loredan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elinor  Loredan" href="/user/show/2504970-elinor-loredan">Elinor  Loredan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2504970-elinor-loredan?shelf=l-m-montgomery">l-m-montgomery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2504970-elinor-loredan?shelf=2010">2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer131067789" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10306729789365938453">The back cover summary is very misleading. It says that Anne begins to worry that Gilbert doesn't love her anymore, and gives the impression that that worry will be a thread throughout the whole book, when really it only takes up the last chapter. <br/>But who's complaining? Not me. I gave it 4 stars, after all.<br/>Still, I don't like misleading back covers...:/ I was actually looking forward to finding out what Anne would come up with to get back Gilbert's love, which she thought she had lost, but nothi</span>
  <span id="freeText10306729789365938453" style="display:none">The back cover summary is very misleading. It says that Anne begins to worry that Gilbert doesn't love her anymore, and gives the impression that that worry will be a thread throughout the whole book, when really it only takes up the last chapter. <br />But who's complaining? Not me. I gave it 4 stars, after all.<br />Still, I don't like misleading back covers...:/ I was actually looking forward to finding out what Anne would come up with to get back Gilbert's love, which she thought she had lost, but nothing very Anneish or exciting happens with it.<br />Also, there are hints of malice in LMM's work that really rather disturbs me. For example, in Ingleside Rilla doesn't like a Sunday School teacher because she is ugly, and Rilla couldn't bear an ugly teacher. <br />That just bugs me. LMM does emphasize looks too much for my taste, but the other aspects of her work are so good they overshadow that complaint.<br />I feel bad for Shirley, who never gets his own chapter about a memorable episode of his childhood. I wanted to get to know him, like the others. <br /><br />As usual, Montgomery creates a beautiful world I long to live in. But then, in a sense I do live in it, by reading about it. </span>
  <a data-text-id="10306729789365938453" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
